# Chat Transcript

## User
Investigate src/main/java/me/winterbelle/prompvault/models/data and check if I'm doing anything wrong with my database models

## Assistant
I inspected the Spring Data JPA model package and related repository/application configuration.

Findings:

- High: `FlaggedPrompt.java` used `@JoinTable(name = "policy_keywords")`, which collided with the real `PolicyKeyword` entity table. Hibernate created `policy_keywords` as both entity table and many-to-many join table, producing columns like `flagged_prompt_id`, `policy_keyword_id`, and `text` in one table. Recommended separate join table: `flagged_prompt_policy_keywords`.
- High: `PromptVaultUser.java` mapped `promptList` with `@JoinColumn(name = "fk_prompt_id")`, while `Prompt.java` already had `fk_account_id`. Hibernate created both columns on `prompts`, including `fk_prompt_id` referencing users. Recommended `@OneToMany(mappedBy = "account", cascade = CascadeType.ALL)`.
- Medium: `PromptHistoryItem.java` had a stray `‰` in the table name: `prompt_history_items‰`. Hibernate really created a quoted table with that character.
- Medium: Enum fields were persisted as ordinals in `Prompt.java` and `PromptVaultUser.java`. Recommended `@Enumerated(EnumType.STRING)`.
- Medium: `application.properties` had `spring.jpa.hibernate.ddl-auto = create`, which drops/recreates tables on startup. Recommended `update` for local prototyping or migrations/`validate` once schema matters.
- Low: `username` and `email` only had indexes, not uniqueness constraints. Recommended DB-level unique constraints.

Verification:

- `./mvnw test` passed after allowing Maven to use `~/.m2` and connect to local MySQL.
- Hibernate DDL confirmed the problems: it created `prompt_history_items‰`, reused `policy_keywords` as a join table, and added both `fk_account_id` and `fk_prompt_id` to `prompts`.

## User
Make those set of fixes for me

## Assistant
Implemented the database model fixes.

Changed:

- `PromptVaultUser.java`: added unique constraints for `username` and `email`.
- `PromptVaultUser.java`: persisted `Role` and `Status` as strings.
- `PromptVaultUser.java`: fixed `promptList` to use `mappedBy = "account"` instead of creating the wrong `fk_prompt_id`.
- `Prompt.java`: persisted `Visibility` as a string.
- `application.properties`: changed Hibernate DDL from `create` to `update` and removed the explicit MySQL dialect.

Note:

- The join table and history table typo were already corrected when reread before editing.
- A sandboxed `./mvnw test` run failed because it could not connect to local MySQL.
- Rerunning `./mvnw test` with local MySQL access passed: build success, 1 test passed.
- Hibernate generated the corrected schema: separate `flagged_prompt_policy_keywords` join table, no extra `fk_prompt_id` on `prompts`, string enum columns, and corrected `prompt_history_items`.

Caveat:

- `ddl-auto=update` will not clean up old bad tables or columns in an existing database. If the local DB still has the previous bad schema, drop it manually or write a migration.

I am building a spring boot webapp to generate and store prompts for a user. This is going to be the home page. It needs to a button in the top right that logs the user out.
The main page should get a list of "prompts" the user has. It can currently just be the private ones for a user (prompts created by the user). Each prompt card should have a button on it that calls "send prompt" A bar on the left side should show and admin section if the user is an admin (session has a UserDetailsObject with "isAdmin()" method). The admin section will have "Manage Users", "Manage Prompt Categories", "Manage Policy Keywords" and "View Flagged Prompts". They can be stubbed for now. A section below should be available for all users to "Create Prompt", "View Prmopts" and "View prompt history".

Based on your current home.html, the cleanest approach is a dashboard layout with:

    Top header

        Application title

        Logged-in user name (optional)

        Logout button (top-right)

    Left sidebar

        Admin section (conditionally displayed)

            Manage Users

            Manage Prompt Categories

            Manage Policy Keywords

            View Flagged Prompts

        User section

            Create Prompt

            View Prompts

            View Prompt History

    Main content area

        List of the user's private prompts

        Each prompt displayed as a card

        "Send Prompt" button on each card

Since your Prompt entity contains title, promptText, and category, those map naturally to the card view.

Your controller would simply provide:

@GetMapping("/home")
public String home(Model model,
                   HttpSession session) {

    UserDetailsObject user =
            (UserDetailsObject) session.getAttribute("userDetailsObject");

    List<Prompt> prompts =
            promptService.findPrivatePromptsForUser(user.getUserId());

    model.addAttribute("prompts", prompts);
    model.addAttribute("userDetailsObject", user);

    return "home";
}

This gives you a usable MVP dashboard that can later be extended with prompt searching, filtering by category, pagination, and public/shared prompts.

Home Page Responsibilities

Keep /home very lightweight:

    Welcome message

    Quick explanation of the application

    Recent activity (later)

    Sidebar navigation

    Logout button

Don't load potentially hundreds of prompts on the dashboard. Users generally expect:

    Dashboard/Home

    Prompts

    Create Prompt

    History

as separate screens.
Suggested Navigation

HOME
├── Dashboard (welcome page)
├── Create Prompt
├── View Prompts
├── Prompt History

ADMIN
├── Manage Users
├── Manage Prompt Categories
├── Manage Policy Keywords
└── View Flagged Prompts

home.html

Something closer to:

<div class="main-content">

    <h1>Welcome to Prompt Vault</h1>

    <p>
        Store, organize, and manage your AI prompts in one place.
    </p>

    <br>

    <p>
        Use the navigation menu on the left to create new prompts,
        browse your existing prompts, or review your prompt history.
    </p>

This keeps the page fast and uncluttered.
Separate Prompt List Page

Create:

templates/

│   ├── list.html
│   └── create.html

Controller:

@GetMapping("/prompts")
public String viewPrompts(Model model) {

    List<Prompt> prompts = promptService.getCurrentUsersPrompts();

    model.addAttribute("prompts", prompts);

    return "prompts/list";
}

Reusable Prompt Card Fragment

I would actually make the cards a Thymeleaf fragment rather than a standalone page.

templates/
├── fragments/
│   └── prompt-card.html

fragments/prompt-card.html

<!DOCTYPE html>
<html xmlns:th="http://www.thymeleaf.org">
<body>

<div th:fragment="promptCard(prompt)"
     class="prompt-card">

    <h3 th:text="${prompt.title}">Title</h3>

    <p th:text="${prompt.category != null ? prompt.category.name : 'No Category'}">
        Category
    </p>

    <p th:text="${prompt.promptText}">
        Prompt Text
    </p>

    <form th:action="@{/prompts/send/{id}(id=${prompt.id})}"
          method="post">

        <button type="submit">
            Send Prompt
        </button>

    </form>

</div>

</body>
</html>

prompts/list.html

<div class="prompt-grid">

    <div th:each="prompt : ${prompts}">

        <div th:replace="~{fragments/prompt-card :: promptCard(${prompt})}">
        </div>

    </div>

</div>

Why use a fragment?

If later you add:

    Prompt search results

    Public prompt browsing

    Category pages

    Admin prompt review

    Flagged prompt review

you can reuse the same card everywhere:

th:replace="~{fragments/prompt-card :: promptCard(${prompt})}"

instead of duplicating the markup across multiple pages.

For a Spring Boot + Thymeleaf application, I'd recommend:

    home.html → dashboard/welcome page

    prompts/list.html → prompt listing

    fragments/prompt-card.html → reusable card component

That's a structure that scales much better as the application grows.

I think I need a prompt service too

Yes. Once you move beyond a simple CRUD controller, a PromptService becomes the right place to keep all prompt-related business logic.

A good separation would be:

Controller
    ↓
PromptService
    ↓
PromptRepository

Repository

public interface PromptRepository extends JpaRepository<Prompt, Long> {

    List<Prompt> findByAccountId(Long accountId);

}

Service Interface

public interface PromptService {

    List<Prompt> getPromptsForUser(Long userId);

    Prompt getPrompt(Long promptId);

    Prompt createPrompt(Prompt prompt);

    void deletePrompt(Long promptId);

    void sendPrompt(Long promptId);
}

Service Implementation

@Service
@Transactional
public class PromptServiceImpl implements PromptService {

    private final PromptRepository promptRepository;

    public PromptServiceImpl(PromptRepository promptRepository) {
        this.promptRepository = promptRepository;
    }

    @Override
    public List<Prompt> getPromptsForUser(Long userId) {
        return promptRepository.findByAccountId(userId);
    }

    @Override
    public Prompt getPrompt(Long promptId) {
        return promptRepository.findById(promptId)
                .orElseThrow(() ->
                        new EntityNotFoundException(
                                "Prompt not found: " + promptId));
    }

    @Override
    public Prompt createPrompt(Prompt prompt) {
        return promptRepository.save(prompt);
    }

    @Override
    public void deletePrompt(Long promptId) {
        promptRepository.deleteById(promptId);
    }

    @Override
    public void sendPrompt(Long promptId) {

        Prompt prompt = getPrompt(promptId);

        // future:
        // open chat window
        // call AI provider
        // log usage
        // save history

        System.out.println(
                "Sending prompt: " + prompt.getTitle());
    }
}

Controller Example

@Controller
@RequestMapping("/prompts")
public class PromptController {

    private final PromptService promptService;

    public PromptController(PromptService promptService) {
        this.promptService = promptService;
    }

    @GetMapping
    public String listPrompts(
            @SessionAttribute UserDetailsObject user,
            Model model) {

        model.addAttribute(
                "prompts",
                promptService.getPromptsForUser(
                        user.getUserId()));

        return "prompts/list";
    }
}

Future-Proof Methods I'd Add Now

Based on the features you've described (private prompts, admin moderation, history, categories), I'd probably start with:

public interface PromptService {

    List<Prompt> getPromptsForUser(Long userId);

    List<Prompt> getPrivatePromptsForUser(Long userId);

    Prompt getPrompt(Long promptId);

    Prompt createPrompt(Prompt prompt);

    Prompt updatePrompt(Prompt prompt);

    void deletePrompt(Long promptId);

    void sendPrompt(Long promptId);
}

Even if some methods initially delegate straight to the repository, having the service layer in place now makes it much easier when you later add:

    ownership validation

    prompt visibility rules

    moderation checks

    policy keyword scanning

    prompt history creation

    AI provider integrations

without bloating your controllers.

We can stub the sendPrompt to be something like "Ai Service responded with 'TODO' at {request-time} for prompt {prompt-text}" but it shouldn't return void

I agree. Returning void makes sense only when the service is performing a side effect and the caller doesn't care about the result.

For your MVP, sendPrompt() is really simulating an AI request, so it should return a response object.
Option 1 (Recommended): Create a DTO

public class PromptResponse {

    private Long promptId;
    private String promptTitle;
    private String promptText;
    private String responseText;
    private LocalDateTime requestTime;

    // getters/setters
}

Service:

@Override
public PromptResponse sendPrompt(Long promptId) {

    Prompt prompt = getPrompt(promptId);

    PromptResponse response = new PromptResponse();

    response.setPromptId(prompt.getId());
    response.setPromptTitle(prompt.getTitle());
    response.setPromptText(prompt.getPromptText());
    response.setRequestTime(LocalDateTime.now());

    response.setResponseText(
        "AI Service responded with 'TODO' at "
            + response.getRequestTime()
            + " for prompt: "
            + prompt.getPromptText()
    );

    return response;
}

Interface:

PromptResponse sendPrompt(Long promptId);

Option 2: Create a Future Prompt History Entity

Since you already plan on having "Prompt History", I'd actually lean toward this design.

Create:

@Entity
public class PromptHistory {

    @Id
    @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.AUTO)
    private Long id;

    @ManyToOne
    private Prompt prompt;

    private LocalDateTime requestTime;

    @Column(length = 5000)
    private String responseText;
}

Then:

PromptHistory sendPrompt(Long promptId);

Implementation:

@Override
public PromptHistory sendPrompt(Long promptId) {

    Prompt prompt = getPrompt(promptId);

    PromptHistory history = new PromptHistory();

    history.setPrompt(prompt);
    history.setRequestTime(LocalDateTime.now());

    history.setResponseText(
        "AI Service responded with 'TODO' at "
            + history.getRequestTime()
            + " for prompt "
            + prompt.getPromptText()
    );

    return promptHistoryRepository.save(history);
}

This has a nice evolution path:

Today:
Prompt -> Fake Response -> History Record

Later:
Prompt -> OpenAI/Claude/Gemini -> History Record

and your "View Prompt History" page becomes trivial:

List<PromptHistory> findByPromptId(Long promptId);

or

List<PromptHistory> findByPromptAccountId(Long userId);

Given that prompt history is already in your requirements, I'd skip the temporary DTO and introduce a PromptHistory entity now. The stubbed response can be stored exactly the same way a real AI response will be stored later, which means almost no refactoring when you integrate an actual LLM.

PromptHistoryItem.java
File
Here's the prompt-history-item we have that we'll want to use

That's actually even better than creating a new history entity—you already have one.

Your existing PromptHistoryItem stores:

    The user/account

    The original prompt text

    The AI response text

which is exactly what your stubbed sendPrompt() needs.

I'd change the service contract to:

PromptHistoryItem sendPrompt(Long promptId);

Then create a repository:

public interface PromptHistoryRepository
        extends JpaRepository<PromptHistoryItem, Long> {

    List<PromptHistoryItem> findByAccountId(Long accountId);

}

And implement:

@Service
@Transactional
public class PromptServiceImpl implements PromptService {

    private final PromptRepository promptRepository;
    private final PromptHistoryRepository promptHistoryRepository;

    public PromptServiceImpl(
            PromptRepository promptRepository,
            PromptHistoryRepository promptHistoryRepository) {

        this.promptRepository = promptRepository;
        this.promptHistoryRepository = promptHistoryRepository;
    }

    @Override
    public PromptHistoryItem sendPrompt(Long promptId) {

        Prompt prompt = getPrompt(promptId);

        PromptHistoryItem historyItem =
                new PromptHistoryItem();

        historyItem.setAccount(prompt.getAccount());

        historyItem.setPromptText(
                prompt.getPromptText());

        historyItem.setResponseText(
                String.format(
                        "AI Service responded with 'TODO' at %s for prompt '%s'",
                        LocalDateTime.now(),
                        prompt.getPromptText()
                ));

        return promptHistoryRepository.save(historyItem);
    }
}

Then your controller becomes:

@PostMapping("/send/{id}")
public String sendPrompt(
        @PathVariable Long id,
        RedirectAttributes redirectAttributes) {

    PromptHistoryItem result =
            promptService.sendPrompt(id);

    redirectAttributes.addFlashAttribute(
            "message",
            result.getResponseText());

    return "redirect:/prompts";
}

A few things I'd consider adding to PromptHistoryItem before you get too far:

private LocalDateTime requestTime;

@ManyToOne
@JoinColumn(name = "fk_prompt_id")
private Prompt prompt;

Right now you're storing only the text snapshot.

That's fine for preserving history, but later you'll probably want to answer questions like:

    Show history for Prompt #42

    Show all executions of a prompt

    Count executions per prompt

    Show latest response for a prompt

Without a direct Prompt relationship, those queries become awkward.

A structure like:

Prompt
   |
   +---- PromptHistoryItem #1
   +---- PromptHistoryItem #2
   +---- PromptHistoryItem #3

is usually what you end up wanting for prompt-management systems. The text fields can remain as snapshots so history is preserved even if the original prompt gets edited later.

If a prompt gets deleted, we want history to remain so we probably don't want to do that yet at least

I think that's a reasonable choice.

In fact, your current PromptHistoryItem design is implicitly following an audit log/snapshot pattern:

Prompt
   ↓ send
PromptHistoryItem
    promptText = snapshot
    responseText = snapshot

Once the history record is created, it no longer depends on the existence of the original prompt.

That gives you a few benefits:

    Users can delete prompts without losing history.

    History remains an immutable record of what was actually sent.

    If a user edits a prompt later, old history entries still show the original text that was used.

    You don't have to worry about cascade-delete configuration.

For example:

Prompt:
"Write me a fantasy story"

User sends it.

History stores:

promptText:
"Write me a fantasy story"

responseText:
"AI Service responded..."

Then the user changes the prompt to:

"Write me a sci-fi story"

or deletes it entirely.

The history still accurately represents what happened at the time of execution.

The only thing I'd consider adding now is a timestamp:

private LocalDateTime requestTime;

because otherwise your "View Prompt History" page can't easily answer:

    When was this run?

    What was the latest run?

    Sort history newest-first

Something like:

@NotNull
private LocalDateTime requestTime;

Then your stub becomes:

@Override
public PromptHistoryItem sendPrompt(Long promptId) {

    Prompt prompt = getPrompt(promptId);

    PromptHistoryItem historyItem =
            new PromptHistoryItem();

    historyItem.setAccount(prompt.getAccount());
    historyItem.setPromptText(prompt.getPromptText());

    LocalDateTime requestTime = LocalDateTime.now();

    historyItem.setRequestTime(requestTime);

    historyItem.setResponseText(
            String.format(
                    "AI Service responded with 'TODO' at %s for prompt '%s'",
                    requestTime,
                    prompt.getPromptText()
            ));

    return promptHistoryRepository.save(historyItem);
}

That keeps the history completely independent from Prompt while still giving you enough information to build a useful history screen later.

I added that to the home but I'm getting this error: 
org.springframework.expression.spel.SpelEvaluationException: EL1011E: Method call: Attempted to call method isAdmin() on null context object
	at org.springframework.expression.spel.ast.MethodReference.nullTargetException(MethodReference.java:235) ~[spring-expression-7.0.7.jar:7.0.7]
	at org.springframework.expression.spel.ast.MethodReference.getValueRef(MethodReference.java:111) ~[spring-expression-7.0.7.jar:7.0.7]
	at

That error means exactly what Spring is saying:

Attempted to call method isAdmin() on null context object

So this expression:

th:if="${userDetailsObject.isAdmin()}"

is being evaluated when userDetailsObject is null.
First thing to check

Add a temporary debug line to your page:

<p th:text="${userDetailsObject}"></p>

If nothing appears, your model isn't getting populated.
Common Cause #1: Not adding it to the model

Your controller needs something like:

@GetMapping("/home")
public String home(Model model,
                   HttpSession session) {

    UserDetailsObject user =
            (UserDetailsObject) session.getAttribute("userDetailsObject");

    model.addAttribute("userDetailsObject", user);

    return "home";
}

If you forgot the model.addAttribute(...), Thymeleaf won't know about it.
Common Cause #2: Session attribute doesn't exist

You may be doing:

session.getAttribute("userDetailsObject");

but the login code stored it under a different name:

session.setAttribute("user", userDetailsObject);

or

session.setAttribute("currentUser", userDetailsObject);

Verify the exact attribute name being used during authentication.
Defensive Thymeleaf Fix

Even after fixing the controller, I would change:

th:if="${userDetailsObject.isAdmin()}"

to:

th:if="${userDetailsObject != null and userDetailsObject.isAdmin()}"

This prevents the page from exploding if the user object is unexpectedly missing.
If You're Using Spring Security

If UserDetailsObject is already the authenticated principal, a cleaner approach is:

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

instead of checking a session object manually.

Or:

<div th:if="${#authentication.principal.admin}">

depending on how your custom UserDetailsObject is implemented.

The next thing I'd look at is where you create/login the user and where userDetailsObject gets stored in the session. If you show that controller/service code, I can pinpoint the exact mismatch in a minute or two.

Here's my security config:
package me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.configs;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.auth.PromptVaultUserDetailsService;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;
import org.springframework.security.authentication.AuthenticationProvider;
import org.springframework.security.authentication.dao.DaoAuthenticationProvider;
import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.builders.HttpSecurity;
import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.configuration.EnableWebSecurity;
import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.configurers.AbstractHttpConfigurer;
import org.springframework.security.crypto.bcrypt.BCryptPasswordEncoder;
import org.springframework.security.crypto.password.PasswordEncoder;
import org.springframework.security.web.SecurityFilterChain;

@Configuration
@EnableWebSecurity
public class SecurityConfig {

    @Bean
    public PasswordEncoder encoder() {
        return new BCryptPasswordEncoder();
    }

    @Bean
    SecurityFilterChain securityFilterChain(HttpSecurity http) {
        return http
                .csrf(AbstractHttpConfigurer::disable)
                .formLogin(httpForm -> {
                    httpForm.loginPage("/req/login").permitAll();
                    httpForm.loginProcessingUrl("/req/login");
                    httpForm.defaultSuccessUrl("/home", true);
                })
                .authorizeHttpRequests(registry -> {
                    registry.requestMatchers("/req/**", "/css/**", "/js/**").permitAll();
                    registry.anyRequest().authenticated();
                })
                .build();
    }

    @Bean
    public AuthenticationProvider authenticationProvider(PromptVaultUserDetailsService userDetailsService, PasswordEncoder passwordEncoder) {
        DaoAuthenticationProvider provider = new DaoAuthenticationProvider(userDetailsService);
        provider.setPasswordEncoder(passwordEncoder);
        return provider;
    }
}

Looking at your SecurityConfig, I don't see anything that would automatically place a userDetailsObject into the model or session.

After login, Spring Security is doing this:

httpForm.defaultSuccessUrl("/home", true);

which means the authenticated user is available through the Spring Security Authentication object, not through:

session.getAttribute("userDetailsObject")

unless you've explicitly stored it there somewhere else.
Most likely what's happening

Your controller probably does something like:

@GetMapping("/home")
public String home(Model model) {
    return "home";
}

and your Thymeleaf page contains:

th:if="${userDetailsObject.isAdmin()}"

Since no model attribute named userDetailsObject exists, Thymeleaf sees:

userDetailsObject == null

and throws the exception.
Better approach with Spring Security

Inject the authenticated principal directly:

@GetMapping("/home")
public String home(
        Model model,
        Authentication authentication) {

    UserDetailsObject user =
            (UserDetailsObject) authentication.getPrincipal();

    model.addAttribute("userDetailsObject", user);

    return "home";
}

This is the most common Spring Security pattern.
Even Better: Use Thymeleaf Security

If your UserDetailsObject exposes authorities/roles, you can avoid passing it into the model entirely.

Add the Thymeleaf security namespace:

<html
    xmlns:th="http://www.thymeleaf.org"
    xmlns:sec="http://www.thymeleaf.org/extras/spring-security">

Then:

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

    <h3>Admin</h3>

    <a href="#">Manage Users</a>
    <a href="#">Manage Prompt Categories</a>
    <a href="#">Manage Policy Keywords</a>
    <a href="#">View Flagged Prompts</a>

</div>

No model attribute required.
If you want to keep isAdmin()

Then your controller should explicitly add it:

@GetMapping("/home")
public String home(
        Model model,
        Authentication authentication) {

    UserDetailsObject user =
            (UserDetailsObject) authentication.getPrincipal();

    model.addAttribute("userDetailsObject", user);

    return "home";
}

and your template should be defensive:

th:if="${userDetailsObject != null and userDetailsObject.isAdmin()}"

I'd be interested in seeing your UserDetailsObject class next. If it already implements Spring Security's UserDetails, there's a very clean way to expose the admin flag and username directly to Thymeleaf without manually copying anything into the model.

I like that approach. This is what my userDetailsObject has:

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.auth;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.enums.Status;
import org.jspecify.annotations.Nullable;
import org.springframework.security.core.GrantedAuthority;
import org.springframework.security.core.userdetails.User;

import java.util.Collection;
import java.util.Objects;

public class UserDetailsObject extends User {

    private final Long userId;
    private final Status status;

    public UserDetailsObject(Long userId, String username, @Nullable String password, Status status, Collection<? extends GrantedAuthority> authorities) {
        super(username, password, authorities);
        this.userId = userId;
        this.status = status;
    }

    public Long getUserId() {
        return userId;
    }

    public Boolean isAdmin() {
        return this.getAuthorities().stream().anyMatch(a -> Objects.requireNonNull(a.getAuthority()).equals("ROLE_ADMIN"));
    }

    public Boolean isNotAdmin() {
        return !isAdmin();
    }

    @Override
    public boolean isEnabled() {
        return this.status == Status.enabled;
    }
}

Your UserDetailsObject is set up nicely for this.

Since it extends Spring Security's User, you can access it directly from the authenticated principal instead of storing it in the session.
Controller

@GetMapping("/home")
public String home(
        Authentication authentication,
        Model model) {

    UserDetailsObject user =
            (UserDetailsObject) authentication.getPrincipal();

    model.addAttribute("user", user);

    return "home";
}

Then your Thymeleaf becomes:

<div th:if="${user.isAdmin()}">

    <h3>Admin</h3>

    <a href="#">Manage Users</a>
    <a href="#">Manage Prompt Categories</a>
    <a href="#">Manage Policy Keywords</a>
    <a href="#">View Flagged Prompts</a>

</div>

Even Better: Use Spring Security Roles Directly

Since isAdmin() is already checking for:

ROLE_ADMIN

I'd actually let Thymeleaf Security do the work.

Add the namespace:

<html
    xmlns:th="http://www.thymeleaf.org"
    xmlns:sec="http://www.thymeleaf.org/extras/spring-security">

Then:

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

    <h3>Admin</h3>

    <a href="#">Manage Users</a>
    <a href="#">Manage Prompt Categories</a>
    <a href="#">Manage Policy Keywords</a>
    <a href="#">View Flagged Prompts</a>

</div>

Now:

    No model attribute required

    No controller code required

    No null checks required

    Spring Security handles everything

You can also show the username in the header:

<span sec:authentication="name"></span>

or if you want your custom principal:

<span th:text="${#authentication.principal.username}"></span>

Since your class extends User, getUsername() is already inherited.
Logout Button

Since you've disabled CSRF:

.csrf(AbstractHttpConfigurer::disable)

this works:

<form th:action="@{/logout}" method="post">
    <button type="submit" class="logout-btn">
        Logout
    </button>
</form>

You don't need any additional logout configuration because Spring Security provides /logout by default.

            <a th:href="@{/admin/flagged}">

            </a>

        </div>

    </sec:authorize>

        <a th:href="@{/prompts/create}">
            Create Prompt
        </a>

        <a th:href="@{/prompts}">
            View Prompts
        </a>

        <a th:href="@{/prompts/history}">
            View Prompt History
        </a>

I think I want to put the user's firstName, lastName, email into the userDetailsObject so I can reference those in the views easily. Is that reasonable?

Yes, that's a very common pattern and I think it's reasonable for your application.

Right now your UserDetailsObject only contains:

private final Long userId;
private final Status status;

which means every time a page wants to display:

    First name

    Last name

    Full name

    Email

you'd need another database lookup.

Instead, when Spring Security authenticates the user, you can load those values once and keep them in the principal for the duration of the session.
I'd extend it like this

public class UserDetailsObject extends User {

    private final Long userId;
    private final String firstName;
    private final String lastName;
    private final String email;
    private final Status status;

    public UserDetailsObject(
            Long userId,
            String username,
            String password,
            String firstName,
            String lastName,
            String email,
            Status status,
            Collection<? extends GrantedAuthority> authorities) {

        super(username, password, authorities);

        this.userId = userId;
        this.firstName = firstName;
        this.lastName = lastName;
        this.email = email;
        this.status = status;
    }

    // getters...
}

Add a convenience method

public String getDisplayName() {

    return firstName + " " + lastName;
}

or slightly safer:

public String getDisplayName() {

    if (firstName == null && lastName == null) {
        return getUsername();
    }

    return String.format("%s %s",
            firstName == null ? "" : firstName,
            lastName == null ? "" : lastName)
            .trim();
}

Then in Thymeleaf

Header:

<span th:text="${#authentication.principal.displayName}">
    User Name
</span>

Email:

<span th:text="${#authentication.principal.email}">
    Email
</span>

Admin check:

<div th:if="${#authentication.principal.admin}">

or

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

What I would NOT put in UserDetailsObject

Things that change frequently or are large:

List<Prompt> prompts;
List<PromptHistoryItem> history;
Set<Role> permissions;

The security principal should stay relatively lightweight.

Good candidates:

id
username
email
firstName
lastName
status
roles/authorities

Bad candidates:

prompt list
history list
preferences object
large profile objects

My favorite version

I often add:

public String getDisplayName() {
    return firstName + " " + lastName;
}

public String getInitials() {
    return String.valueOf(firstName.charAt(0))
            + String.valueOf(lastName.charAt(0));
}

Then your header can eventually become:

<div class="user-chip">
    <div class="avatar"
         th:text="${#authentication.principal.initials}">
        JD
    </div>

    <div>
        <div th:text="${#authentication.principal.displayName}">
            Jane Doe
        </div>

        <small th:text="${#authentication.principal.email}">
            jane@example.com
        </small>
    </div>
</div>

which fits very nicely with the purple-themed dashboard you're building.

Add JavaScript Before </body>

<script>

    const username = document.getElementById('username');
    const email = document.getElementById('email');
    const firstName = document.getElementById('firstName');
    const lastName = document.getElementById('lastName');
    const password = document.getElementById('password');
    const passwordConfirmation =
        document.getElementById('passwordConfirmation');

    const registerButton =
        document.getElementById('registerButton');

    const passwordMessage =
        document.getElementById('passwordMatchMessage');

    function validateForm() {

        const allFieldsFilled =
            username.value.trim() !== '' &&
            email.value.trim() !== '' &&
            firstName.value.trim() !== '' &&
            lastName.value.trim() !== '' &&
            password.value !== '' &&
            passwordConfirmation.value !== '';

        const passwordsMatch =
            password.value === passwordConfirmation.value;

        if (
            passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
            !passwordsMatch
        ) {

            passwordMessage.textContent =
                'Passwords do not match';

            passwordMessage.className =
                'validation-message error';

        } else if (
            passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
            passwordsMatch
        ) {

            passwordMessage.textContent =
                'Passwords match';

            passwordMessage.className =
                'validation-message success';

        } else {

            passwordMessage.textContent = '';
            passwordMessage.className =
                'validation-message';

        }

        registerButton.disabled =
            !(allFieldsFilled && passwordsMatch);
    }

    [
        username,
        email,
        firstName,
        lastName,
        password,
        passwordConfirmation
    ].forEach(field => {

        field.addEventListener(
            'input',
            validateForm
        );

    });

</script>

Still Validate On The Server

Even with this JavaScript, keep checks in your registration controller/service:

if (!password.equals(passwordConfirmation)) {
    throw new IllegalArgumentException(
            "Passwords do not match");
}

and verify:

username not already used
email not already used
password meets requirements

because users can always bypass browser-side JavaScript. The JavaScript improves UX; the server validation enforces correctness.

<script>

    const username = document.getElementById('username');
    const email = document.getElementById('email');
    const firstName = document.getElementById('firstName');
    const lastName = document.getElementById('lastName');
    const password = document.getElementById('password');
    const passwordConfirmation =
        document.getElementById('passwordConfirmation');

    const registerButton =
        document.getElementById('registerButton');

    const passwordMessage =
        document.getElementById('passwordMatchMessage');

    function validateForm() {

        const allFieldsFilled =
            username.value.trim() !== '' &&
            email.value.trim() !== '' &&
            firstName.value.trim() !== '' &&
            lastName.value.trim() !== '' &&
            password.value !== '' &&
            passwordConfirmation.value !== '';

        const passwordsMatch =
            password.value === passwordConfirmation.value;

        if (
            passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
            !passwordsMatch
        ) {

            passwordMessage.textContent =
                'Passwords do not match';

            passwordMessage.className =
                'validation-message error';

        } else if (
            passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
            passwordsMatch
        ) {

            passwordMessage.textContent =
                'Passwords match';

            passwordMessage.className =
                'validation-message success';

        } else {

            passwordMessage.textContent = '';

            passwordMessage.className =
                'validation-message';

        }

        registerButton.disabled =
            !(allFieldsFilled && passwordsMatch);
    }

    [
        username,
        email,
        firstName,
        lastName,
        password,
        passwordConfirmation
    ].forEach(field => {

        field.addEventListener(
            'input',
            validateForm
        );

    });

</script>

</body>
</html>

I agree. "Passwords match" doesn't add much value. The enabled button is already the positive feedback.

I'd simplify it to:
JavaScript

Replace:

if (
    passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
    !passwordsMatch
) {

    passwordMessage.textContent =
        'Passwords do not match';

    passwordMessage.className =
        'validation-message error';

} else if (
    passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
    passwordsMatch
) {

    passwordMessage.textContent =
        'Passwords match';

    passwordMessage.className =
        'validation-message success';

} else {

    passwordMessage.textContent = '';

    passwordMessage.className =
        'validation-message';

}

with:

if (
    passwordConfirmation.value.length > 0 &&
    !passwordsMatch
) {

    passwordMessage.textContent =
        'Passwords do not match';

    passwordMessage.className =
        'validation-message error';

} else {

    passwordMessage.textContent = '';

    passwordMessage.className =
        'validation-message';

}

CSS

You can also remove the success styling entirely:

.validation-message {
    margin-bottom: 15px;
    font-size: .9rem;
    font-weight: 600;
}

.validation-message.error {
    color: #dc2626;
}

This creates a cleaner UX:

    User starts typing → no message

    Passwords differ → red "Passwords do not match"

    Passwords match → message disappears and button enables

That's usually how modern registration forms behave.

It looks liek for my user has the "user" role but the sidebar is rendering the admin section. Is the authorities not being set on the role properly? Also it should always default to not turning up anyway

If a user with the USER role is seeing the admin section, there are a few likely causes.
First: What does your sidebar condition actually look like?

If you're using:

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

then a normal user should never see that section.

If you're using:

<div th:if="${#authentication.principal.admin}">

or

<div th:if="${#authentication.principal.isAdmin()}">

then we need to verify what isAdmin() is returning.
Check the Authorities Being Loaded

Your isAdmin() method is:

public Boolean isAdmin() {
    return this.getAuthorities().stream()
            .anyMatch(a ->
                    Objects.requireNonNull(a.getAuthority())
                            .equals("ROLE_ADMIN"));
}

That logic is fine.

The real question is: what authorities are being passed into the constructor?

Somewhere in PromptVaultUserDetailsService you likely have code like:

return new UserDetailsObject(
    user.getId(),
    user.getUsername(),
    user.getPasswordHash(),
    user.getStatus(),
    authorities
);

I would temporarily log them:

authorities.forEach(a ->
    System.out.println("Authority: " + a.getAuthority()));

or

System.out.println(authorities);

during login.

If you see:

ROLE_ADMIN

for a normal user, the issue is in the authority mapping.
Check Your Role Enum

I suspect your database role might be:

USER
ADMIN

and you're converting it like:

new SimpleGrantedAuthority("ROLE_" + role.name())

which is correct.

But if you're accidentally doing:

List.of(
    new SimpleGrantedAuthority("ROLE_USER"),
    new SimpleGrantedAuthority("ROLE_ADMIN")
)

for every user, everyone becomes an admin.
Add a Debug Section to Home

Temporarily add:

<p>
    User:
    <span th:text="${#authentication.name}"></span>
</p>

<p>
    Authorities:
    <span th:text="${#authentication.authorities}"></span>
</p>

A normal user should see something like:

Authorities:
[ROLE_USER]

An admin should see:

Authorities:
[ROLE_ADMIN]

or

[ROLE_USER, ROLE_ADMIN]

depending on your design.
Make It Fail Closed

You're absolutely right that it should default to hidden.

I prefer:

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

because if:

    authentication is null

    principal is null

    authorities are missing

then the block simply doesn't render.

Whereas:

th:if="${#authentication.principal.isAdmin()}"

depends on your custom code executing correctly.

My guess is the bug is not in Thymeleaf or isAdmin(). It's probably in PromptVaultUserDetailsService where you're building the Collection<? extends GrantedAuthority> and accidentally giving everyone ROLE_ADMIN.

If you paste that service, I can probably spot the issue immediately.

I think I see the problem.

For Thymeleaf Security, the namespace declaration should be:

xmlns:sec="http://www.thymeleaf.org/extras/spring-security"

or for newer versions:

xmlns:sec="https://www.thymeleaf.org/extras/spring-security"

The value itself usually doesn't affect rendering much, but your bigger concern is whether the security dialect is actually installed.
Quick Test

Add this somewhere visible:

<p sec:authentication="name">anonymous</p>

If you see your username, the security dialect is working.

If you literally see:

<p sec:authentication="name">

or nothing happens, the dialect isn't active.

</div>

That would explain why your admin menu always appears.
Most Common Cause

You have:

xmlns:sec="https://www.thymeleaf.org/thymeleaf-extras-springsecurity6"

but you may be missing the dependency.

For Spring Boot 3 / Spring Security 6 you need:

<dependency>
    <groupId>org.thymeleaf.extras</groupId>
    <artifactId>thymeleaf-extras-springsecurity6</artifactId>
</dependency>

Without that dependency, Thymeleaf treats:

<sec:authorize access="hasRole('ADMIN')">

as just another HTML element and renders its contents.

That would produce exactly the behavior you're seeing: every user sees the admin section.
Easiest Verification

Open your page source in the browser.

If you see:

<sec:authorize access="hasRole('ADMIN')">

in the generated HTML, then the dialect isn't processing at all.

If the dialect is working, the <sec:authorize> tag never reaches the browser.

My bet is that thymeleaf-extras-springsecurity6 is either:

    Missing from your dependencies, or

    Not being picked up by Spring Boot.

Can you show your pom.xml (or Gradle dependencies)? That's the next place I'd look.

Here's the user service:
package me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.auth;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data.PromptVaultUser;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.services.PromptVaultUserService;
import org.jspecify.annotations.NonNull;
import org.springframework.security.core.authority.SimpleGrantedAuthority;
import org.springframework.security.core.userdetails.UserDetails;
import org.springframework.security.core.userdetails.UserDetailsService;
import org.springframework.security.core.userdetails.UsernameNotFoundException;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;

import java.util.List;
import java.util.Optional;

@Service
public class PromptVaultUserDetailsService implements UserDetailsService {
    final private PromptVaultUserService promptVaultUserService;

    PromptVaultUserDetailsService(PromptVaultUserService promptVaultUserService) {
        this.promptVaultUserService = promptVaultUserService;
    }

    @Override
    @NonNull
    public UserDetails loadUserByUsername(@NonNull String username) throws UsernameNotFoundException {
        final Optional<PromptVaultUser> maybeUser = promptVaultUserService.findUser(username);
        if (maybeUser.isPresent()) {
            final PromptVaultUser user = maybeUser.get();
            return new UserDetailsObject(user.getId(),
                    user.getUsername(),
                    user.getPasswordHash(),
                    user.getFirstName(),
                    user.getLastName(),
                    user.getEmail(),
                    user.getStatus(),
                    List.of(
                            new SimpleGrantedAuthority("ROLE_" + user.getRole().name().toUpperCase()
                            )
                    )
            );
        }
        throw UsernameNotFoundException.fromUsername(username);
    }
}

Your authority creation code actually looks correct:

List.of(
    new SimpleGrantedAuthority(
        "ROLE_" + user.getRole().name().toUpperCase()
    )
)

So if the database role is USER, the authority becomes:

ROLE_USER

and hasRole('ADMIN') should evaluate to false.

What jumps out at me is the way the sidebar is written.

You're using:

<sec:authorize access="hasRole('ADMIN')">

This is the old element/tag style. With Spring Security 6 and the Thymeleaf extras dialect, the most reliable pattern is usually the attribute form:

<div class="sidebar-section"
     sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">

    <h3>Administration</h3>

    <a th:href="@{/admin/users}">
        Manage Users
    </a>

    <a th:href="@{/admin/categories}">
        Manage Prompt Categories
    </a>

    <a th:href="@{/admin/keywords}">
        Manage Policy Keywords
    </a>

    <a th:href="@{/admin/flagged}">
        View Flagged Prompts
    </a>

</div>

I'd change the sidebar to that form first.

public enum Role {

    ADMIN
}

then you're fine.

But if somehow user.getRole() is returning ADMIN for everyone due to a registration bug or default value, then every user will receive:

new SimpleGrantedAuthority("ROLE_ADMIN")

and Thymeleaf is behaving correctly.

If it prints:

I am building a springboot web-app with a mysql database and a thymeleaf front-end.
I've attached my database objects. 
One model, has a manual requestTime.
I would like to instead have each object have an automatic createdAt and updatedAt timestamp.

Yes. In a Spring Boot + JPA/Hibernate application, the usual approach is to replace manually managed timestamps (like your requestTime) with automatic audit fields:

    createdAt → set once when the row is inserted

    updatedAt → updated automatically whenever the row changes

For example, your current PromptHistoryItem has:

@NotNull
private LocalDateTime requestTime;

You can replace it with:

import org.hibernate.annotations.CreationTimestamp;
import org.hibernate.annotations.UpdateTimestamp;

private LocalDateTime createdAt;

private LocalDateTime updatedAt;

and annotate them:

@CreationTimestamp
@Column(nullable = false, updatable = false)
private LocalDateTime createdAt;

@UpdateTimestamp
@Column(nullable = false)
private LocalDateTime updatedAt;

Option 1 (Recommended): Create a Base Entity

Since you said you'd like every object to have these fields, create a shared superclass.
AuditableEntity.java

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data;

import jakarta.persistence.Column;
import jakarta.persistence.MappedSuperclass;
import org.hibernate.annotations.CreationTimestamp;
import org.hibernate.annotations.UpdateTimestamp;

import java.time.LocalDateTime;

@MappedSuperclass
public abstract class AuditableEntity {

    @CreationTimestamp
    @Column(nullable = false, updatable = false)
    private LocalDateTime createdAt;

    @UpdateTimestamp
    @Column(nullable = false)
    private LocalDateTime updatedAt;

    public LocalDateTime getCreatedAt() {
        return createdAt;
    }

    public LocalDateTime getUpdatedAt() {
        return updatedAt;
    }
}

Then extend it
Prompt

@Entity
@Table(name = "prompts")
public class Prompt extends AuditableEntity {

PromptVaultUser

@Entity
@Table(name = "prompt_vault_users")
public class PromptVaultUser extends AuditableEntity {

PromptCategory

@Entity
@Table(name = "prompt_categories")
public class PromptCategory extends AuditableEntity {

PolicyKeyword

@Entity
@Table(name = "policy_keywords")
public class PolicyKeyword extends AuditableEntity {

FlaggedPrompt

@Entity
@Table(name = "flagged_prompts")
public class FlaggedPrompt extends AuditableEntity {

PromptHistoryItem

@Entity
@Table(name = "prompt_history_items")
public class PromptHistoryItem extends AuditableEntity {

and remove:

private LocalDateTime requestTime;

along with its getter/setter.
Database Migration

Hibernate will generate two new columns for each table:

created_at DATETIME NOT NULL,
updated_at DATETIME NOT NULL

If you're using Flyway, a migration for prompt_history_items would look like:

ALTER TABLE prompt_history_items
    ADD COLUMN created_at DATETIME NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    ADD COLUMN updated_at DATETIME NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP;

For existing rows, you may want:

UPDATE prompt_history_items
SET created_at = NOW(),
    updated_at = NOW()
WHERE created_at IS NULL;

Repeat for the other tables.
Alternative: Spring Data JPA Auditing

If you want framework-level auditing rather than Hibernate-specific annotations, Spring Data supports:

@CreatedDate
private LocalDateTime createdAt;

@LastModifiedDate
private LocalDateTime updatedAt;

with:

@EnableJpaAuditing

in a configuration class.

For your project, since you're already using Hibernate/JPA entities directly, @CreationTimestamp and @UpdateTimestamp are the simplest and most common solution.

One additional improvement: in PromptHistoryItem, consider renaming promptText and responseText columns to TEXT in MySQL if responses can be long:

@Column(columnDefinition = "TEXT")
private String responseText;

Otherwise MySQL may default to a shorter VARCHAR length depending on your schema generation settings.

can i put the ID in the base auditableEntity class too?

Yes, absolutely. In fact, if every entity in your application uses the same primary key strategy, putting the id in the base class is a common pattern.

Your AuditableEntity would become something like:

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data;

import jakarta.persistence.*;
import org.hibernate.annotations.CreationTimestamp;
import org.hibernate.annotations.UpdateTimestamp;

import java.time.LocalDateTime;

@MappedSuperclass
public abstract class AuditableEntity {

    @Id
    @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.AUTO)
    private Long id;

    @CreationTimestamp
    @Column(nullable = false, updatable = false)
    private LocalDateTime createdAt;

    @UpdateTimestamp
    @Column(nullable = false)
    private LocalDateTime updatedAt;

    public Long getId() {
        return id;
    }

    public LocalDateTime getCreatedAt() {
        return createdAt;
    }

    public LocalDateTime getUpdatedAt() {
        return updatedAt;
    }
}

Then your entities become much cleaner:

@Entity
@Table(name = "prompts")
public class Prompt extends AuditableEntity {

    @ManyToOne
    @JoinColumn(name = "fk_account_id")
    private PromptVaultUser account;

    // other fields...
}

and:

@Entity
@Table(name = "policy_keywords")
public class PolicyKeyword extends AuditableEntity {

    private String text;

}

No need to repeat:

@Id
@GeneratedValue(...)
private Long id;

in every class.
A few considerations

This works well because all of your uploaded entities currently use:

Long id

with auto-generated values. Looking at your models, I don't see any entity that needs a different key type or composite key, so it's a good fit.
One improvement I'd make

Use a consistent generation strategy everywhere. Right now some classes use:

@GeneratedValue

and others use:

@GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.AUTO)

If the ID moves into the base class, pick one strategy and use it consistently.

For MySQL, I usually prefer:

@GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)

because it maps directly to MySQL's AUTO_INCREMENT.

@Id
@GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
private Long id;

That tends to be the most predictable choice with MySQL.
Result

Your entities shrink to mostly business fields:

@Entity
@Table(name = "prompt_history_items")
public class PromptHistoryItem extends AuditableEntity {

    @ManyToOne
    @JoinColumn(name = "fk_account_id")
    private PromptVaultUser account;

    @NotBlank
    private String promptText;

    @NotBlank
    private String responseText;
}

which is exactly the kind of cleanup a base entity is meant to provide.

First, what model are you?
I am building a springboot web-app with a mysql database and a thymeleaf front-end.

I need to add a "manage prompt categories" page. I have a button for it in my sidebar but currently it does nothing. Only admin users should be able to see this page. Admin users should see a list of all prompt categories. The admin should be able to add, edit, delete a prompt category. When admin tries to save a prompt category we must sanitise the input and ensure it's an admin user who is calling these endpoints

First: I am GPT-5.5.

Looking at your uploaded files, you already have:

    A PromptCategory entity with a name field.

    A sidebar link pointing to /admin/categories.

    Admin-only sidebar visibility using sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')".

For a secure Spring Boot implementation, I'd structure it like this:
1. Create an Admin Controller

Even though the menu item is hidden in the UI, never rely on the UI for security.

@Controller
@RequestMapping("/admin/categories")
@PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")
public class AdminPromptCategoryController {

    private final PromptCategoryService promptCategoryService;

    public AdminPromptCategoryController(
            PromptCategoryService promptCategoryService) {
        this.promptCategoryService = promptCategoryService;
    }

    @GetMapping
    public String list(Model model) {
        model.addAttribute(
                "categories",
                promptCategoryService.findAll());

        model.addAttribute(
                "newCategory",
                new PromptCategoryDto());

        return "admin/categories";
    }

    @PostMapping
    public String create(
            @Valid @ModelAttribute("newCategory")
            PromptCategoryDto dto) {

        promptCategoryService.create(dto);
        return "redirect:/admin/categories";
    }

    @PostMapping("/{id}/update")
    public String update(
            @PathVariable Long id,
            @Valid PromptCategoryDto dto) {

        promptCategoryService.update(id, dto);
        return "redirect:/admin/categories";
    }

    @PostMapping("/{id}/delete")
    public String delete(
            @PathVariable Long id) {

        promptCategoryService.delete(id);
        return "redirect:/admin/categories";
    }
}

2. Use a DTO Instead of Binding Directly to the Entity

public class PromptCategoryDto {

    @NotBlank
    @Size(max = 100)
    private String name;

    // getters/setters
}

This prevents mass-assignment issues and gives you validation.
3. Sanitize User Input

Create a utility:

@Component
public class InputSanitizer {

    public String sanitize(String input) {

        if (input == null) {
            return null;
        }

        return Jsoup.clean(
                input,
                Safelist.none())
                .trim();
    }
}

Add JSoup dependency:

<dependency>
    <groupId>org.jsoup</groupId>
    <artifactId>jsoup</artifactId>
</dependency>

Then in the service:

@Service
@Transactional
public class PromptCategoryService {

    private final PromptCategoryRepository repository;
    private final InputSanitizer sanitizer;

    public PromptCategoryService(
            PromptCategoryRepository repository,
            InputSanitizer sanitizer) {

        this.repository = repository;
        this.sanitizer = sanitizer;
    }

    public void create(PromptCategoryDto dto) {

        PromptCategory category = new PromptCategory();

        category.setName(
                sanitizer.sanitize(dto.getName()));

        repository.save(category);
    }
}

This strips HTML, scripts, and other markup.
4. Validate Duplicates

Add a repository method:

public interface PromptCategoryRepository
        extends JpaRepository<PromptCategory, Long> {

    boolean existsByNameIgnoreCase(String name);
}

Then:

String name = sanitizer.sanitize(dto.getName());

if (repository.existsByNameIgnoreCase(name)) {
    throw new IllegalArgumentException(
            "Category already exists");
}

5. Protect the Endpoints

You said:

    ensure it's an admin user who is calling these endpoints

Do this in two layers.
Controller layer

@PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")

Security configuration

.requestMatchers("/admin/**")
.hasRole("ADMIN")

This ensures that even if someone manually browses to:

/admin/categories

they get denied unless they are an admin.

</table>

7. Add Editing

For editing, either:
Option A (simplest)

Open a separate edit page:

GET /admin/categories/{id}/edit
POST /admin/categories/{id}/edit

Option B

Inline editing with a text box per row.

For maintainability, I recommend the separate edit page.
8. Additional Hardening

Since this is an admin feature, I'd also add:
CSRF protection

Spring Security enables this by default.

Make sure forms include:

<input type="hidden"
       th:name="${_csrf.parameterName}"
       th:value="${_csrf.token}" />

(or use Thymeleaf's Spring Security integration that injects it automatically).
Audit logging

Since your entity already extends AuditableEntity, consider logging:

log.info(
    "Admin {} created category {}",
    username,
    category.getName());

for create/update/delete actions.
Recommended architecture

PromptCategory
PromptCategoryDto
PromptCategoryRepository
PromptCategoryService
AdminPromptCategoryController
admin/categories.html
admin/category-edit.html
InputSanitizer

This keeps validation, sanitization, authorization, and persistence concerns separated and makes the feature easy to test and maintain.

Viewing all categories should not be admin only - users will be able to view categories for assigning to prompts later. It's create/update/delete actions (write-actions) that are admin only. 

Correct — make read access general, and protect only write actions.

Use this pattern:

@Controller
@RequestMapping("/categories")
public class PromptCategoryController {

    private final PromptCategoryService service;

    public PromptCategoryController(PromptCategoryService service) {
        this.service = service;
    }

    @GetMapping
    public String list(Model model) {
        model.addAttribute("categories", service.findAll());
        model.addAttribute("newCategory", new PromptCategoryDto());
        return "categories/index";
    }

    @PostMapping
    @PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")
    public String create(@Valid @ModelAttribute PromptCategoryDto dto) {
        service.create(dto);
        return "redirect:/categories";
    }

    @PostMapping("/{id}/update")
    @PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")
    public String update(
            @PathVariable Long id,
            @Valid @ModelAttribute PromptCategoryDto dto) {

        service.update(id, dto);
        return "redirect:/categories";
    }

    @PostMapping("/{id}/delete")
    @PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")
    public String delete(@PathVariable Long id) {
        service.delete(id);
        return "redirect:/categories";
    }
}

Then your security config should allow reads but restrict writes:

.requestMatchers(HttpMethod.GET, "/categories", "/categories/**")
.authenticated()

.requestMatchers(HttpMethod.POST, "/categories/**")
.hasRole("ADMIN")

In Thymeleaf, show the list to everyone, but only show forms/buttons to admins:

<h1>Prompt Categories</h1>

<table>
    <tr>
        <th>Name</th>
        <th sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">Actions</th>
    </tr>

    <tr th:each="category : ${categories}">
        <td th:text="${category.name}"></td>

        <td sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">
            <form th:action="@{/categories/{id}/delete(id=${category.id})}"
                  method="post">
                <button type="submit">Delete</button>
            </form>
        </td>
    </tr>
</table>

<div sec:authorize="hasRole('ADMIN')">
    <h2>Add Category</h2>

    <form th:action="@{/categories}" method="post"
          th:object="${newCategory}">
        <input type="text" th:field="*{name}">
        <button type="submit">Add</button>
    </form>
</div>

And update your sidebar link from:

<a th:href="@{/admin/categories}">

to:

<a th:href="@{/categories}">

So the rule becomes:

    GET /categories → authenticated users can view

    POST /categories → admin only

    POST /categories/{id}/update → admin only

    POST /categories/{id}/delete → admin only

Keep sanitisation in the service, not the controller, so every write path is protected consistently.

Yes — keep the page admin-only, but expose category data through the service for later prompt creation.

Your current SecurityConfig disables CSRF and only requires authentication for everything except /req/**, /css/**, /js/**. I’d update it like this.
Updated SecurityConfig

@Configuration
@EnableWebSecurity
@EnableMethodSecurity
public class SecurityConfig {

    @Bean
    public PasswordEncoder encoder() {
        return new BCryptPasswordEncoder();
    }

    @Bean
    SecurityFilterChain securityFilterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
        return http
                .formLogin(httpForm -> {
                    httpForm.loginPage("/req/login").permitAll();
                    httpForm.loginProcessingUrl("/req/login");
                    httpForm.defaultSuccessUrl("/home", true);
                })
                .authorizeHttpRequests(registry -> {
                    registry.requestMatchers("/req/**", "/css/**", "/js/**").permitAll();

                    registry.requestMatchers("/admin/**").hasRole("ADMIN");

                    registry.anyRequest().authenticated();
                })
                .build();
    }

    @Bean
    public AuthenticationProvider authenticationProvider(
            PromptVaultUserDetailsService userDetailsService,
            PasswordEncoder passwordEncoder) {

        DaoAuthenticationProvider provider =
                new DaoAuthenticationProvider(userDetailsService);

        provider.setPasswordEncoder(passwordEncoder);
        return provider;
    }
}

Important changes:

@EnableMethodSecurity

and remove:

.csrf(AbstractHttpConfigurer::disable)

PromptCategoryRepository

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.repositories;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data.PromptCategory;
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.JpaRepository;

public interface PromptCategoryRepository
        extends JpaRepository<PromptCategory, Long> {

    boolean existsByNameIgnoreCase(String name);

    boolean existsByNameIgnoreCaseAndIdNot(String name, Long id);
}

PromptCategoryDto

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.dtos;

import jakarta.validation.constraints.NotBlank;
import jakarta.validation.constraints.Size;

public class PromptCategoryDto {

    @NotBlank(message = "Category name is required")
    @Size(max = 100, message = "Category name must be 100 characters or fewer")
    private String name;

    public PromptCategoryDto() {
    }

    public PromptCategoryDto(String name) {
        this.name = name;
    }

    public String getName() {
        return name;
    }

    public void setName(String name) {
        this.name = name;
    }
}

InputSanitizer

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.security;

import org.jsoup.Jsoup;
import org.jsoup.safety.Safelist;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component
public class InputSanitizer {

    public String sanitizePlainText(String input) {
        if (input == null) {
            return null;
        }

        return Jsoup.clean(input, Safelist.none()).trim();
    }
}

Dependency:

<dependency>
    <groupId>org.jsoup</groupId>
    <artifactId>jsoup</artifactId>
    <version>1.17.2</version>
</dependency>

PromptCategoryService

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.services;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data.PromptCategory;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.dtos.PromptCategoryDto;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.repositories.PromptCategoryRepository;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.security.InputSanitizer;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.transaction.annotation.Transactional;

import java.util.List;

@Service
public class PromptCategoryService {

    private final PromptCategoryRepository repository;
    private final InputSanitizer sanitizer;

    public PromptCategoryService(
            PromptCategoryRepository repository,
            InputSanitizer sanitizer) {

        this.repository = repository;
        this.sanitizer = sanitizer;
    }

    public List<PromptCategory> findAll() {
        return repository.findAll();
    }

    public PromptCategory findById(Long id) {
        return repository.findById(id)
                .orElseThrow(() -> new IllegalArgumentException("Category not found"));
    }

    @Transactional
    public void create(PromptCategoryDto dto) {
        String name = sanitizer.sanitizePlainText(dto.getName());

        if (name.isBlank()) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category name is required");
        }

        if (repository.existsByNameIgnoreCase(name)) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category already exists");
        }

        PromptCategory category = new PromptCategory();
        category.setName(name);

        repository.save(category);
    }

    @Transactional
    public void update(Long id, PromptCategoryDto dto) {
        PromptCategory category = findById(id);

        String name = sanitizer.sanitizePlainText(dto.getName());

        if (name.isBlank()) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category name is required");
        }

        if (repository.existsByNameIgnoreCaseAndIdNot(name, id)) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category already exists");
        }

        category.setName(name);
        repository.save(category);
    }

    @Transactional
    public void delete(Long id) {
        repository.deleteById(id);
    }
}

This service can later be reused in your prompt-create controller:

model.addAttribute("categories", promptCategoryService.findAll());

AdminPromptCategoryController

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.controllers.admin;

import jakarta.validation.Valid;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.dtos.PromptCategoryDto;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.services.PromptCategoryService;
import org.springframework.security.access.prepost.PreAuthorize;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Controller;
import org.springframework.ui.Model;
import org.springframework.validation.BindingResult;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.*;

@Controller
@RequestMapping("/admin/categories")
@PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")
public class AdminPromptCategoryController {

    private final PromptCategoryService service;

    public AdminPromptCategoryController(PromptCategoryService service) {
        this.service = service;
    }

    @GetMapping
    public String index(Model model) {
        model.addAttribute("categories", service.findAll());
        model.addAttribute("newCategory", new PromptCategoryDto());
        return "admin/categories/index";
    }

    @PostMapping
    public String create(
            @Valid @ModelAttribute("newCategory") PromptCategoryDto dto,
            BindingResult bindingResult,
            Model model) {

        if (bindingResult.hasErrors()) {
            model.addAttribute("categories", service.findAll());
            return "admin/categories/index";
        }

        service.create(dto);
        return "redirect:/admin/categories";
    }

    @GetMapping("/{id}/edit")
    public String edit(@PathVariable Long id, Model model) {
        var category = service.findById(id);

        model.addAttribute("categoryId", id);
        model.addAttribute("category", new PromptCategoryDto(category.getName()));

        return "admin/categories/edit";
    }

    @PostMapping("/{id}/edit")
    public String update(
            @PathVariable Long id,
            @Valid @ModelAttribute("category") PromptCategoryDto dto,
            BindingResult bindingResult) {

        if (bindingResult.hasErrors()) {
            return "admin/categories/edit";
        }

        service.update(id, dto);
        return "redirect:/admin/categories";
    }

    @PostMapping("/{id}/delete")
    public String delete(@PathVariable Long id) {
        service.delete(id);
        return "redirect:/admin/categories";
    }
}

We should have a PromptCategoryService as an interface and the implementation can be PromptCategoryServiceImpl

Agreed. Split it like this.
PromptCategoryService

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.services;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data.PromptCategory;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.dtos.PromptCategoryDto;

import java.util.List;

public interface PromptCategoryService {

    List<PromptCategory> findAll();

    PromptCategory findById(Long id);

    void create(PromptCategoryDto dto);

    void update(Long id, PromptCategoryDto dto);

    void delete(Long id);
}

PromptCategoryServiceImpl

package me.winterbelle.prompvault.services.impl;

import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.data.PromptCategory;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.models.dtos.PromptCategoryDto;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.repositories.PromptCategoryRepository;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.services.PromptCategoryService;
import me.winterbelle.prompvault.utils.helpers.security.InputSanitizer;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.transaction.annotation.Transactional;

import java.util.List;

@Service
public class PromptCategoryServiceImpl implements PromptCategoryService {

    private final PromptCategoryRepository repository;
    private final InputSanitizer sanitizer;

    public PromptCategoryServiceImpl(
            PromptCategoryRepository repository,
            InputSanitizer sanitizer) {

        this.repository = repository;
        this.sanitizer = sanitizer;
    }

    @Override
    public List<PromptCategory> findAll() {
        return repository.findAll();
    }

    @Override
    public PromptCategory findById(Long id) {
        return repository.findById(id)
                .orElseThrow(() -> new IllegalArgumentException("Category not found"));
    }

    @Override
    @Transactional
    public void create(PromptCategoryDto dto) {
        String name = sanitizer.sanitizePlainText(dto.getName());

        if (name.isBlank()) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category name is required");
        }

        if (repository.existsByNameIgnoreCase(name)) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category already exists");
        }

        PromptCategory category = new PromptCategory();
        category.setName(name);

        repository.save(category);
    }

    @Override
    @Transactional
    public void update(Long id, PromptCategoryDto dto) {
        PromptCategory category = findById(id);

        String name = sanitizer.sanitizePlainText(dto.getName());

        if (name.isBlank()) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category name is required");
        }

        if (repository.existsByNameIgnoreCaseAndIdNot(name, id)) {
            throw new IllegalArgumentException("Category already exists");
        }

        category.setName(name);
        repository.save(category);
    }

    @Override
    @Transactional
    public void delete(Long id) {
        repository.deleteById(id);
    }
}

Your controller still injects the interface:

    this.service = service;

Let's also make a nicer error page. It currently looks like this:

Yes — do both:

    Create a cute purple fallback error page

Update SecurityConfig

import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.method.configuration.EnableMethodSecurity;

@Configuration
@EnableWebSecurity
@EnableMethodSecurity
public class SecurityConfig {

    @Bean
    public PasswordEncoder encoder() {
        return new BCryptPasswordEncoder();
    }

    @Bean
    SecurityFilterChain securityFilterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
        return http
                .formLogin(httpForm -> {
                    httpForm.loginPage("/req/login").permitAll();
                    httpForm.loginProcessingUrl("/req/login");
                    httpForm.defaultSuccessUrl("/home", true);
                })
                .exceptionHandling(exception -> {
                    exception.accessDeniedHandler((request, response, accessDeniedException) -> {
                        response.sendRedirect("/home");
                    });
                })
                .authorizeHttpRequests(registry -> {
                    registry.requestMatchers("/req/**", "/css/**", "/js/**", "/images/**").permitAll();
                    registry.requestMatchers("/admin/**").hasRole("ADMIN");
                    registry.anyRequest().authenticated();
                })
                .build();
    }

    @Bean
    public AuthenticationProvider authenticationProvider(
            PromptVaultUserDetailsService userDetailsService,
            PasswordEncoder passwordEncoder) {

        DaoAuthenticationProvider provider =
                new DaoAuthenticationProvider(userDetailsService);

        provider.setPasswordEncoder(passwordEncoder);
        return provider;
    }
}

This means a logged-in non-admin visiting /admin/categories gets sent to /home.

            font-weight: bold;
        }

        .home-button {
            display: inline-block;
            margin-top: 1.5rem;
            padding: 0.8rem 1.4rem;
            border-radius: 999px;
            background: #8b5cf6;
            color: white;
            text-decoration: none;
            font-weight: bold;
        }

        .home-button:hover {
            background: #7c3aed;
        }
    </style>
</head>

    <div class="error-card">

        <div class="error-icon">🌙</div>

        <div class="error-code">
            Error <span th:text="${status}">500</span>
        </div>

            This page seems to have wandered off into the prompt vault.
        </p>

        <p th:if="${status != 404}">
            Something unexpected happened, but don’t worry — your prompts are still safe.
        </p>

        <a class="home-button"
           th:href="@{/home}">
            Take me home
        </a>

    </div>
</div>

</body>
</html>

Place it here:

src/main/resources/templates/error.html

That gives you a nicer fallback page, while normal forbidden admin access quietly redirects to /home.

I already have an app.css you made previously. I should just be able to re-use the colurs from that, right?

Absolutely. In fact, that's the better approach.

The inline styles I showed were just to demonstrate the layout, but for a real application you should keep all colours, spacing, border radii, shadows, button styles, etc. in app.css.

Something like:

<div class="error-page">
    <div class="error-card">

        <div class="error-icon">🌙</div>

        <h1>Oopsie, something went wonky</h1>

        <div class="error-code">
            Error <span th:text="${status}">500</span>
        </div>

        <p>
            Something unexpected happened, but don't worry — your prompts are still safe.
        </p>

        <a class="btn-primary"
           th:href="@{/home}">
            Take me home
        </a>

    </div>
</div>

Then in app.css reuse your existing variables/classes:

.error-page {
    min-height: 100vh;
    display: flex;
    justify-content: center;
    align-items: center;
}

.error-card {
    background: var(--card-background);
    border: 1px solid var(--border-colour);
    border-radius: var(--border-radius);
    box-shadow: var(--card-shadow);
}

If your CSS already has things like:

:root {
    --primary-purple: ...;
    --secondary-purple: ...;
    --background-colour: ...;
}

then definitely use those instead of introducing new hard-coded colours.

One thing I'd recommend since Prompt Vault already has a header and sidebar layout:

For 404s and 500s, consider rendering the error page inside the normal application shell:

<div th:replace="~{fragments/header :: header}"></div>

<div class="content">

    <div th:replace="~{fragments/sidebar :: sidebar}"></div>

    <div class="main-content">

        <div class="error-card">
            ...
        </div>

    </div>

</div>

That way users stay inside the application experience rather than being thrown onto a completely separate-looking page.

For the admin-access case though, I'd still redirect directly to /home rather than showing an error page. From a UX perspective:

    User enters admin URL manually

    System says "Nope"

    User lands back on Home

That's cleaner than showing a 403 page for a situation they'll likely hit accidentally.
